1 PREFACE 
This project has been developed in connection with the completion of a degree in Civil Engineering, 
Building Technology. The project is a Master of science (MSc) project corresponding to 30 ECTS points. It 
has been developed in the period February 2017 to June 2017. The project is written in cooperation with 
the department of civil engineering at Technical University of Denmark (DTU) with the architectural 
materials supplied by engineering consulting company MOE A/S. 
We are very thankful to the company MOE A/S for the permission to use their materials.  
We are also very thankful to our supervisor, Arne Egerup, for his dedicated guidance throughout the 
project. His guidance and experience has been a great factor in the completion of our project.  
 
Kongens Lyngby, 30. June, 2017 
  
Wen Jun Wang & Wisam Elias Neaman  
 
  
 
==PAGE:3==
Side 3 af 292 
 
 
2 ABSTRACT 
Due to the architectural profession development, a constant flow of ideas for design proposals arise, which 
means that buildings today are becoming more and more complicated, requiring the implementation of 
special solutions. 
The choice of this thesis is based on its comprehensive nature of the content, allowing for an all-round 
documentation of various different, special solutions.  
This thesis presents the development of a building’s structural design based on an architectural project.  
The scope of the project is to create a structural solution of two seven-storey concrete buildings, a student 
housing and a nursing home, located on Mariedalsvej 55-57 in central Frederiksberg. The buildings have a 
modern architectural design and a usage, which coincide with the very open and modern environment that 
represents the overall surrounding theme of Frederiksberg. To ensure the safety of the buildings, the 
project is carried out in accordance with Eurocode using the Danish national annexes and is presented in 
the form of a static documentation.  
Based on the architectural layout, the first phases of the structural design are to establish the static system 
and to classify the loads acting on the structural elements. The structural elements consist of walls, beams, 
columns and floor decks. The design of the structural elements is made, so that the walls both act as the 
stabilizing system, which resists the horizontal loads, and as a bearing system together with the beams, 
columns and floor decks, carrying all the vertical loads. 
The architectural layout of the buildings also includes a retracted facade at the ground floor and an open 
roof terrace on one of the upper floors for both the buildings. Both spots required a specially designed 
bearing and stabilizing system for them to be possible. For the retracted facade, a beam layout was 
constructed to carry the out-hanging facade from above, and for the roof terrace a stabilizing system was 
made to support the facade, which extends above any supporting system, against the overturning wind 
force.  
The last phase of this thesis was to develop an overview of all the structural element types and to design 
the joint connections between these.

4 INTRODUCTION 
The project on Mariendalsvej is a building complex consisting of two building Blocks with a common 
parking basement. The building complex is primarily used as apartments for students and elderly. The 
buildings are located in central Frederiksberg in a residential area called Stjernen with easy access to 
shopping, entertainment, health care and much more. This means that the usage of the buildings is very 
compatible with the environment surrounding them.  
Part of the outside area of the buildings is accessible to the public in the form of a café and a fitness center 
at the ground floor. Furthermore, the facade is made in classical bricks, adding to the overall culture, 
environment and open theme of Frederiksberg. 
The shape and geometry of the buildings are almost identical, both with a L-shape geometry and retracted 
facades on the ground floor. The retracted facade might lead to design challenges due to the eccentric 
loading from the facade. Overall, because of this retracted facade the implementation of special joint 
solutions might be needed instead of standard solutions.  
The buildings will be constructed by using both concrete and steel elements. The structural members are 
designed to be prefabricated elements, however in some situations, a special designed element is required.  
The design of the buildings will be done following a standard static documentation layout, where the 
description of general structure, vertical load distribution, horizontal load distribution, stability analysis and 
the verification of the structural elements are presented. StruSoft FEM-design, WinBeam (beam modeling 
program) and excel worksheet are used during the design phase. 
Due to the project’s comprehensive nature of the content, the project allows for an all-round 
documentation of several different, special solutions. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:10==
Side 10 af 292 
 
 
5 PROJECT BASIS 
5.1 GENERAL 
5.1.1 
Building type and usage 
The project consists of two concrete high-rise buildings up to seven storeys located at Mariendalsvej 55-57. 
The buildings are referred to as East and West Block, respectively no. 55 and 57.  
East Block functions as a nursing home for the elderly and comprises of an area of approximately 5200 m2, 
which contains a 130 m2 cafeteria and 48 apartments. 
West Block functions as a dorm for students and comprises of an area of approximately 4500 m2, which 
contains a 150 m2 fitness center and 38 apartments divided into 175 rooms including the roof terrace. 
Both East and West Block share a common basement used as a parking facility. However, the two buildings 
are considered two separate constructions. 
5.1.2 
Main structure system 
The geometry of the buildings is presented in Table 1. 
Table 1: the geometry of the buildings 
 
West Block 
East Block 
Height [m] 
24.43 
23.60 
Width(north side) [m] 
36.56

Width(east side) [m] 
36.17 
41.45 
 
The structural members consist of bearing and stabilizing walls, beams and columns. The beams are divided 
into edge beams that carry the facade, and beams inside the building area, either carrying the slabs or, in 
special cases, the walls. The walls both stabilize the buildings against horizontal loads, and also carry the 
vertical loads, where the columns and the beams only carry the vertical loads. 
The main structure layout from the basement to the roof will be presented in Figure 1 - Figure 7.

5.2 BASIS OF STRUCTURE 
5.2.1 
Norms and standards 
Following norms and standards are used throughout this project: 
• 
Eurocode 0 - Basis of structural design EN 1990-2002, [1]. 
• 
Eurocode 1 - Actions on structures - Part 1-1 General actions - Densities, self-weight, imposed loads 
for buildings, [2]. 
• 
Eurocode 1 - Actions on structures - Part 1-2 General actions - Actions on structures exposed to fire, 
[3]. 
• 
Eurocode 1 - Actions on structures - Part 1-3 General actions - Snow load, [4]. 
• 
Eurocode 1: Actions on structures- Part 1-4: General actions- Wind actions, [5]. 
• 
Eurocode 2 Design of concrete structures - Part 1-2 General rules - Structural fire design, [6]. 
• 
Eurocode 3 - Design of steel structures - Part 1-1 General rules and rules for buildings, [7]. 
• 
Eurocode 3 - Design of steel structures - Part 1-8 Design of joints, [8]. 
• 
JFJ 2010 Betonelementbyggeriets static, [9]. 
• 
Henvisning fra Betonelementbyggeriers statik - Skivebygningers static, [10]. 
• 
Eksempelsamling om brandsikring af byggeri 2012 2. reviderede udgave 2016, [11]. 
• 
Bygningsrereglement 2010 [12] 
• 
Eurocode 1: Actions on structures – Part 1-7: General actions – Accidental actions, [13] 
• 
Teknisk Ståbi 22. Udgave, [14]. 
• 
Eurocode 0 - Basis of structural design, [15]. 
5.2.2 
Safety 
Due to a considerable high risk of human casualties in the event of a possible structural failure, the 
consequence class is primarily chosen to 3, which results in a KFI factor of 1.1 used for the verification of the 
load combinations in accordance with DS/EN 1990 (see Table 2). For some building parts, the consequence 
class should be chosen as 2, but the design of these structures are done by the suppliers and will not be 
considered in this project.  
5.2.3 
ICT-tools 
The following programs have been used throughout the project: 
• 
Excel:  
o Hand calculations through own set up spreadsheets. 
o Diverse existing spreadsheets for structural element verification. 
• 
AutoCad: 2D drawing program. 
• 
Revit: 2D and 3D drawing program. 
• 
Strusoft: FEM design program for structural analysis. 
• 
AcroPlot Matrix: Editing existing PDF drawings. 
• 
WinBeam: Static calculation program. 
• 
Worksheets from betonforeningen1.

Side 20 af 292 
 
 
5.3 CONSTRUCTION CRITERIA 
5.3.1 
Static description 
5.3.1.1 
Main bearing system 
The bearing system of the buildings consists of beams and slabs, which work together as a single disc that 
transfers all the vertical load to the bearing walls and columns and further down to the foundation. 
The floor decks used are prefabricated pre-tensioned hollow core slabs, which span on top prefabricated 
concrete walls and beams. 
5.3.1.2 
Main stabilizing system 
To stabilize the buildings against wind, horizontal mass loads and geometrical imperfections, the floor 
decks will transfer the disc loads to the stabilizing walls and further down to the foundation. All loads are 
transferred to the stable earth layer. The building is secured against possible overturning and sliding, using 
respectively vertical tension reinforcement, and shear locks between the floor decks and the stabilizing 
walls. Furthermore, the self-weight of the buildings also works as a stabilizing force. 
5.3.2 
Working life of construction 
In accordance with DS/EN 1990, the working life of the buildings is 50 years. 
5.3.3 
Robustness 
In accordance with DS/EN 1990 [1] chapter 2.1.4, the structure is designed to avoid damage by events such 
as explosions, impacts or consequence of human error. 
It is specified in DS/EN 1990 [1] that potential damage should be avoided or limited to a localized impact, 
which does not affect the rest of the structure. Suitable materials, design and execution should help to 
prevent this. See DS/EN 1990 [1] chapter 2.1.5-2.1.6 for full list of criteria. 
A design strategy to increase the robustness of the structure needs to be made. The structural bearing 
columns and walls are therefore considered as key elements to prevent failure. This means that the key 
structural bearing elements have additional continuous vertical reinforcement throughout the entire height 
of the building. 
 
==PAGE:21==
Side 21 af 292 
 
 
5.3.4 
Fire safety 
The structural resistance is designed to be adequate for the required period in the case of fire. See DS/EN 
1990 [1] chapter 2.1.3. 
In accordance to BR-10 [12] chapter 5.1.1, the usage category 4 is used. 
Using a collection of fire-safety examples [11], the design of the structural members should be verified in 
accordance to the following criteria (see Table 3). 
Table 3: Fire requirements for the structural members 
All floors, including the basement 
Walls 
REI 120 A2-s1, d0 
Columns & Beams 
REI 120 A2-s1, d0 
Floor decks 
REI 120 A2-s1, d0 
Roof area 
Walls 
REI 60 A2-s1, d0 
Columns & Beams 
REI 60 A2-s1, d0 
Floor decks 
REI 60 A2-s1, d0 
 
REI is the timeframe, where the three criteria Resistance, Entegrity and Isolation are met: 
R: Resistance – bearing capacity. 
E: Entegrity – The building parts’ ability to keep out flames and gas. 
I: Isolation – The building parts’ ability to isolate the heat from the fire.

5.5.3 
Imposed load 
The recommended characteristic values of the imposed loads can be viewed in [2] section 6. 
Recommended values of Ψ factors for buildings can be found in [1] Annex A1. 
Imposed loads are not considered, when dealing with situations of snow and wind, due to less frequenting 
of people in areas where it snows, such as balconies. 
5.5.4 
Snow load 
Snow load is calculated as following: 
𝑠= 𝜇𝑖∙𝐶𝑒∙𝐶𝑡∙𝑠𝑘 
where, 
𝜇𝑖  
is the shape factor for snow load 
𝐶𝑒  
is the exposure factor, generally it is equal to 1.0. 
𝐶𝑡  
is the thermal factor, generally it is equal to 1.0. 
𝑠𝑘  
is a location dependent characteristic terrain value. In Denmark it is set to 1,0 kN/m2. 
5.5.5 
Wind load 
 
Wind load is calculated according to EN 1991-1-4:2005 
𝐹𝑤= 𝑐𝑠∙𝑐𝑑∙𝑐𝑓∙𝑞𝑝(𝑧𝑒) ∙𝐴𝑟𝑒𝑓 
Where, 
𝑐𝑠∙𝑐𝑑  
is the structural factor 
𝑐𝑓  
is the facre coefficient for the structure or structural element 
𝑞𝑝(𝑧𝑒)  
is the peak velocity pressure at reference height 𝑧𝑒 
𝐴𝑟𝑒𝑓  
is the reference area of the structure

5.5.6 
Accident loads 
5.5.6.1 
Impact load case 
In accordance to DS/EN 1991-1-7 [13] section 4. 
Category of traffic:  
Two categories of traffic are considered, when designing the structural elements against impact loads for 
Mariendalsvej 55-57. 
1. The basements bearing elements are designed against the impact of a car. 
Category - Courtyards and parking garages: 
Cars: 
Force Fdxa = 50 kN 
Force Fdya = 25 kN 
2.The ground floor’s bearing elements are designed against the impact of traffic loads in urban areas. This is 
only considered for the bearing elements on the northern side, facing the road. 
Category – Roads in urban area: 
Traffic: 
Force Fdxa = 500 kN 
Force Fdya = 250 kN 
5.5.6.2 
Fire load case 
The fire load design is based on DS/EN 1991-1-2 [3]. 
5.5.6.3 
Explosion load case 
It is deemed unnecessary to take an internal explosion case into account due to the lack of gas installations.  
5.5.6.4 
Seismic load case 
Horizontal mass load is created by any vertical load present in the buildings and is assumed a fixed action 
that can act in any horizontal direction. 
The design value of the horizontal mass load, Ad, considers the geometrical imperfection (See DS/EN 1990 
[15] page 7 NOTE 1). It is calculated based on the conversion from vertical load as follows: 
𝐴𝑑= 1.5% ∙(∑𝐺𝑘,𝑗+ ∑𝛹𝐸,𝑖∙𝑄𝑘,𝑖
𝑖≥1
) 
𝛹𝐸,𝑖 = 𝜑∙𝛹2,𝑖 is the action combination factor in the event of earthquake and horizontal mass load [15]. 
𝜑 
is the reduction factor of the 𝛹-factor and is set to 1, although for imposed loads of category 
A – C it is normally assumed as 0.5 in accordance with EN 1998.  
𝛹2,𝑖  
is chosen from Teknisk Ståbi [14] p. 144. 
The conversion from vertical load to horizontal mass load already incorporates a geometrical imperfection, 
but as an additional safety precaution it is chosen to add an extra 0.25% to the horizontal mass load 
calculation.

5.6 ELEMENT NAMES 
The chosen names of the structural members will be presented in this section and are used to gain an 
overview of all the structural elements. 
• 
Edge beam names should be read as “Orientation_Floor_Facade_Number”. 
For example, “W_0_N_EB1” means “West Block_Ground Floor_North Facade_Edge Beam 1”. 
• 
Beams inside the building area are named as following “Orientation_Floor_Number”. 
For example, “E_2_B3” means “East Block_2nd Floor_Beam 3”. 
• 
Beam names in the basement area are named as following “Basement_Module line along 
span_Module line of start and end of the beam”. 
For example, “B_03_VD-VB” means “Basement_Span along module line 03_The beam spans from 
module line VD to VB”. 
• 
Column names should be read as “Element type_Floor_Module line 1_Module line 2”. 
For example, “Column_B_03_VD” means “Column_Basement_Module line 03_Module line VD”. 
See Figure 8 for illustration. 
 
Figure 8: Illustration of column name 
• 
The stabilizing walls are named as following “V_Module line + floor deck span (a, b, c, ...etc)”. 
For example “V_33a” means wall located at module line 33 with one floor deck span. However, 
“V_33b” is another wall at the same module line, but with a different floor deck span.

6 VERTICAL LOAD DISTRIBUTION 
In this section, the method of the vertical load calculation is presented. 
6.1 LOAD DIAGRAM 
To efficiently separate the usage and load intensity of each room on each floor, an overview of the different 
vertical loads on each floor have been created for both West and East Block. See the load diagram for the 
ground floor in Figure 12. 
The load diagram for all storeys can be seen in Appendix G.
==PAGE:34==
Side 34 af 292 
 
 
 
Figure 12: Load Diagram – Ground floor
==PAGE:35==
Side 35 af 292 
 
 
6.2 CALCULATED MODEL 
The principle of the vertical load distribution can be seen in Figure 13.  
All the floor decks are considered discontinuous and simply supported, which means that two bearing lines 
next to each other take up the load evenly from the floor deck spanning between them. 
Based on this principle and the load combination from Table 8, an excel sheet is constructed specially, to 
make it easy to deal with large amounts of calculation work. 
 
Figure 13: Illustration of the vertical load distribution 
When calculating the load combinations for each structural member, one matrix was setup containing all 
load indexes for the surface loads and one vector containing all load types of the bearing lines. All are 
classified with numbers indexes (see Table 10 and Table 11), which are the numbers used in the worksheet 
under “Load type [-]” and “Bearing line type [-]”.  
==PAGE:36==
Side 36 af 292 
 
 
In Figure 14, an example for calculating the vertical load distribution of a wall as a bearing line is presented. 
A load specification 11 (Gathering room area) is used on the left side with a span of 8.6 m to the next 
bearing line, meanwhile a load specification 9 (Apartment area) is used on the right side with a span of 9.08 
m to the next bearing line. A bearing line with an index 3 (Wall, t = 200 mm) and a corresponding height of 
the storey is entered. 
 
Figure 14: Worksheet input example 
In Figure 15, the worksheet's output is presented for the previous example and an additional explanation of 
the calculation based on the calculation model in Figure 13. The result from the 16 load combinations are 
calculated individually for each floor, and the sum of these are presented at bottom row “Sum of Loads 
[kN/m]”, where the result from maximum load combinations on each floor is marked with a red color font.  
 
Figure 15: Worksheet output example 
The results of the vertical load distribution for the other structural elements can be seen in Appendix D. 
==PAGE:37==
Side 37 af 292 
 
 
7 HORIZONTAL LOAD DISTRIBUTION 
7.1 WIND LOAD CALCULATION 
Due to the geometrical L-shape of the buildings, the two longest sides are where the most critical wind load 
will be investigated. This means that the wind load from the north and east will be considered in the 
following calculations. 
7.1.1 
Terrain categories 
Mariendalsvej 55-57 is located in the middle of the city, where the buildings are surrounded by other 
buildings in such a distance that the terrain categories can be classified as terrain IV. See satellite picture in 
Figure 16.  
 
 
 
Figure 16 – Terrain categories surrounding Mariendalsvej 55-57 
 
7.1.2 
Basis wind pressure 
The basis wind pressure is calculated according to EC1 part 1-4 [5].  An existing basis wind pressure 
calculation sheet is used2. The heights of the buildings are measured to 24.4 m and 23.6 m, respectively 
West and East Block. Notice that rather than measuring the exact area the wind pressure affects, a more 
conservative measurement was made so that the actual height along the building is substituted by the 
height of highest point, assuring an upper limit calculation of the wind pressure. 
                                                          
2 Wind calculation sheet is taken from engineering company MOE A/S. 
==PAGE:38==
Side 38 af 292 
 
 
 
Figure 17 – Basis wind pressure calculation output 
 
 
 
==PAGE:39==
Side 39 af 292 
 
 
7.1.3 
External pressure coefficients of the building 
The external pressure coefficients of the buildings are determined according to EC1 part 1-4 [5].  
Both the West and East Block are almost identical in height, but the East Block is slightly wider than the 
West Block, which results in East Block being the most critical case in the wind calculation. See the 
geometry of the buildings in Table 1. 
However, due to the different layout and geometrical properties of the walls the most critical wind does 
not result in the most critical structural member. The design of the elements will therefore be done for 
both West and East Block separately.  
The results of the wind calculations on the East Block will be presented in this section. The calculation of 
the West Block can be found in Appendix E. 
The ratio between the height of the East Block and the width of the building is found to 0.56.  By linear 
interpolation, the corresponding values for each zone are found in Table 12.

both blocks in the case of wind.  
The horizontal mass load will be calculated and compared to this wind load, and the most critical of the two 
will be the final design load for the structural members.

From Table 16 and  
Table 18 the largest horizontal mass load intensity is present on West Block with an approximate average 
intensity of 0.22 kN/m2. According to “Betonelementbyggeriers statik [9]” p. 77, a rule of thumb estimation 
of the horizontal mass load is made for an apartment building to 0.15 kN/m2 and for a commercial building 
to 0.20 kN/m2. Mariendalsvej 55-57 is a combination of apartments and commercial usage on the ground 
floor. The difference between the rule of thumb and the actual calculated value can be explained by a 
couple of factors. An additional geometrical imperfection load has been applied to the calculations of the 
horizontal mass load, making the intensity approximately 15% larger. Furthermore, the calculation of the 
horizontal mass load has been done in a conservative manner, which is on purpose for both simplification 
and safety. 
However, the largest intensity does not result in the most critical total horizontal mass load. From  
Table 17 and  
Table 19 it can be seen that the most critical total horizontal mass load is found on the East Block, although 
the largest load intensity is found on the West Block. This is due to a larger floor area of the East Block. 
7.3 SUB-CONCLUSION: HORIZONTAL LOAD 
The most critical horizontal load is found by comparing the wind load with the horizontal mass load and 
determining the largest value. 
By comparing section [7.1] Wind load calculation and section [7.2] Horizontal mass load it is concluded 
that the horizontal mass load will be the load used for the design of the structural members. 
7.4 LATERAL LOAD ACTIONS ON STABILIZING SYSTEM 
Generalized model method will be applied for the distribution of the horizontal load actions on the 
stabilizing system. Following assumptions will be made: 
• 
Perfectly stiff floor discs. 
• 
The deformation of the stabilizing walls is proportional to the horizontal load. 
• 
The mass load is distributed evenly along the floor discs. 
• 
The relative stiffness of each wall is a rough estimate according to “Betonelementbyggeriers statik 
[9]”.  
• 
The relative stiffness is taken as a rough estimate and mainly used as a comparison between 
stiffness of the walls. 
• 
Only the walls, which go through the building to the terrain, are considered stabilizing walls. 
• 
The facade at roof terrace is stabilized by the stiffening system, see section [8.4.2] Stiffening 
system of the facade on roof terrace. 
• 
The room to the left of the roof terrace on the 4th floor of the west Block and the room to the right 
of the roof terrace on the 5th floor of the East Block are both stabilized by frame structures. 
7.4.1 
Reaction on the stabilizing walls 
 
According to the generalized method described in “Betonelementbyggeriets statik [9] chapter 4 
‘Hovedstabilitet’ section 3”, the reaction acting on the stabilizing walls has been found.  
==PAGE:44==
Side 44 af 292 
 
 
Since the horizontal mass load is the dominating design load, it is converted into an evenly distributed line 
load along the floor decks for each floor. The horizontal evenly distributed line load will be carried by the 
stabilizing walls based on their placement, relative rotational stiffness and relative stiffness. Furthermore, 
an asymmetric layout of the stabilizing walls create a torsional moment, which is also taken into account, 
when calculating the reactions acting on the stabilizing walls. 
Concerning potential openings in the stabilizing walls, assumptions were made such as: 
• 
Opening areas, which make up less than 25% of the wall area, are neglected under the assumption 
that they have been reinforced around the opening making them stiff enough for the entire wall to 
work as a single disc. 
• 
Opening areas, which make up more than 25% of the wall area, will be considered a gap dividing 
the wall. 
In addition, for walls with larger openings (>25%) modeling in FEM-design would be a recommended 
option. However, in this project, only one stabilizing wall in the East Block’s southern gable exceeded the 
25% opening area, but with a negligible amount and was therefore designed as a single disc. 
The reactions on each wall can be seen in Appendix H. 
 
 
==PAGE:45==
Side 45 af 292 
 
 
7.4.2 
Horizontal reactions on stabilizing walls 
The FEM-design program, StruSoft, has been applied to investigate the compression zone of the stabilizing 
walls due to the horizontal load. The most critical load from the compression zone will be compared to the 
largest load on the stabilizing walls from the vertical load distribution. The design of the reinforcement of the 
stabilizing walls is based on the largest of these two loads. 
There are two types of stabilizing walls of 200 mm and 150 mm, but the most frequently used is the 200 
mm stabilizing walls. The 150 mm stabilizing walls have mainly been used in the southern gable of the 
buildings. In this section, only one of each type of wall will be presented. The entirety of the investigation 
can be seen in Appendix I. 
In Figure 20, the method of calculating the size of compression zone is presented. The normal forces and 
moments are obtained from StruSoft and will be used to determine the size of the compression zone. 
 
 
Figure 20: Schema of the compression zone 
The variables are defined as follows: 
N’ 
is the total vertical load from the elements needed to stabilize the overturn of horizontal 
load. 
M’ 
is the overturning moment of the stabilizing wall caused by the horizontal load. 
e 
is the eccentricity of the total vertical load N’ from the center of the wall. 
c 
is the width of the compression zone. 
 
The following section will present the results from FEM-design of the compression zone on one of each wall 
type. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:46==
Side 46 af 292 
 
 
7.4.3 
Compression zone of stabilizing walls using FEM 
The Figure 21 and Figure 22 show the selected stabilizing walls, which have been modeled and calculated in 
the FEM-program, StruSoft. The selected walls are marked with a circle. However, only the ones marked 
with blue circles are presented in the following section. All the stabilizing walls have a thickness of 200 mm 
except the walls located on the most southern facade of both Blocks, module lines VB and ØA (V_VBa, 
V_VBb, V_VBc and V_ØAa).  
In the modeling of the selected stabilizing walls, openings have been neglected due to an earlier 
assumption made in [7.4.1] Reaction on the stabilizing walls. 
 
 
Figure 21: Selected stabilizing walls West Block

7.4.3.3 
Sub-conclusion: Compression zone of stabilizing walls using FEM 
Four walls (two from each direction) have been analyzed for each building, and from the stabilizing schema 
the two most unstable walls (highest stabilizing utilization) have previously been presented. Although the 
most unstable walls are presented, none of these results in stabilizing issues, which could require extra 
vertical tension reinforcement. 
From Figure 25 and Figure 29 the width and intensity of the compression zone can be seen. The width of 
the compression zone is reduced at the bottom of the wall, which is due to more overturning moment 
being present. 
The vertical load increases almost linearly due to a similar structure on each floor, while the increase in the 
overturning moment has a greater impact on destabilizing the wall due to the increase in moment arm. 
Therefore, the eccentricity is continually increasing from the top of the wall to the bottom. A larger 
eccentricity results in a smaller compression zone and thereby a larger intensity. 
Had the stabilizing utilization been higher, for example 80%, the use of corrugated tubes would have been 
needed to stabilize the structure against overturning.   
Based on the geometry of the walls and the horizontal load, a sufficient stability of the stabilizing walls is 
confirmed. As a result, there is no demand for vertical tension reinforcement in the form of corrugated 
tubes through the stabilizing walls. According to “Bygningsberegninger” [16] p. 253, buildings of 5+ storeys 
should implement vertical tension reinforcement, even though it is not needed as a further safety 
precaution against collapse of the floor decks. Based on this, this project has applied a vertical tension 
reinforcement in the form of Ø80 corrugated tubes and Y16 reinforcements.

Side 53 af 292

As can be seen in Table 23, the final deflection is larger than the allowable deflection (
1
400 ∙𝑠𝑝𝑎𝑛) in 
accordance with DS/EN 1990. This is due to the standard camber being too low, allowing for the deflection 
caused by the characteristic load to deflect more than allowed. Alternatively, an increase in the amount of 
reinforcement in the hollow core slab could be a viable option, where the reinforcement would be 
increased from 3 L9.3 + 6 L12.5 to 9 L12.5, which would increase the standard camber flev to 28.9 mm from 
21 mm for a 9 m span. This would result in a final deflection of 17.61 mm, which is an allowable deflection. 
However, this is deemed unnecessary since the only problem is the deflection for the fitness area and not 
the bearing capacity (See Table 22). Instead a more economic choice is made that requires a higher camber 
from the supplier. This can be done because the final deflection and the allowable deflection are relatively 
close, and an assumption of the camber to be equal to 3 ‰ of the span length is made as a reasonable 
adjustment instead of the standard camber, which is given in the hollow core slabs data table from the 
supplier. The re-verification of the deflection control is made based on these assumptions. See Table 24 for 
the results.

8.2 WALLS 
In the following section, the wall design will be presented. The load used to design the walls is obtained 
from the largest load from either the compression zone intensity calculation or the vertical load 
distribution. In this project, the vertical load distribution was found to be the most critical load case.  
This load will be used as an input data in a wall reinforcement calculation worksheet created by 
“Betonforeningen4”. 
It is assumed that net reinforcement used in the walls have the same amount of vertical and horizontal 
reinforcement. In special cases, where a huge amount of reinforcement in a wall is required, from an 
economic aspect a reduction in horizontal reinforcement in the wall can be considered. This is done since 
the horizontal reinforcement is used to resist the bending of the wall, yet the wall is in most cases stiff 
enough in itself to resist the bending due to the typical height of a wall.  
All the net reinforcement in the walls will be closed using U-shaped stirrups. 
According to the supplier, the minimal distance the slab must overlap the wall is 65 mm. To make sure that 
this is possible, the concretes compression strength needs to be investigated in this situation. The largest 
compression stress obtained from the slabs is far below the designed compression strength of the walls, 
which is min. 25 MPa. In cases where the slab span on both sides of a wall, which is 200 mm thick, the 
overlapping distance between the wall and slab is set to be 90 mm. 
In Figure 34, an overview of the wall thickness is shown. All walls with a thickness of 180 mm are neither 
stabilizing nor bearing, which means that they have not been designed in this project. Instead, a minimum 
reinforcement amount is implemented. 
 
Figure 34: Wall thickness overview 
                                                          
4 BEF.dk  ”Vægarmeringsberegningsark”. 
==PAGE:63==
Side 63 af 292 
 
 
Reinforcement around holes has not been designed in this project. Instead, an illustration of the principle 
of how reinforcement around holes is presented. 
Additional horizontal reinforcement, called lintel, will be applied above and below the hole area. 
Meanwhile, the added vertical reinforcement acting like columns will be implemented at the sides of the 
hole. The lintel works as a beam reinforcement, which resists a bending moment caused by the load above. 
It is primarily a lintel above the hole, which is used, but in certain situations a lintel below a hole is needed 
to reduce a large load on the above lintel. 
 
In Figure 35, an illustration of scenario 1 shows how much load area the lintel at the top of a hole carries if 
there is no lintel below the hole.  
 
Figure 35: Illustration of the load area with only a lintel above the hole (Scenario 1) 
In Figure 36, an illustration of scenario 2 is presented, where the hole is reinforced both above and below 
the hole. 
 
Figure 36: Illustration of the load area with both a lintel above and below the hole (Scenario 2) 
The scale of the load amount in the statical systems in Figure 35 and Figure 36 is proportional.  
See Appendix K (Column type 3) for additional vertical reinforcement location in the wall.   
==PAGE:64==
Side 64 af 292 
 
 
8.2.1 
Wall with thickness of 150 mm 
The wall V_ØAa is the most critically loaded wall with a thickness of 150 mm (see Figure 34 for the 
location). The design load is calculated to be 153 kN/m (see section [12.4.1.23] V_ØAa, page 172). An 
additional estimated load of 100 kN from the roof has been applied. 
To resist this load, the wall is designed to apply a net reinforcement of Y12/150 in both directions and both 
sides for before, and after a 120 min fire duration (see Figure 37 and Figure 38).

8.3 BEAMS & COLUMNS 
8.3.1 
Beams & columns type overview 
The following illustration in Figure 41 shows an overview of the different types of beams and columns used 
for the structural system on the ground floor. For all original A3 size illustrations of the type overview see 
Appendix K. The design load of the beam and column elements can be found in Appendix D. 
A beam verification overview can be seen in Table 29. At certain elements, the deflection exceeds the 
allowable range of L/400 (marked with red font). However, these are still assumed to be acceptable since 
the deviation is relatively small and a reduction of the requirement can be discussed5. 
Steel profiles are widely used in this project to reduce the height of the structure and ultimately gain as 
much floor height as possible, since the floor heights are low to begin with.  
Among the steel profiles used as beams, the following types have been chosen: 
• 
HEB- and HEM – profiles: Mostly used as edge beam 
 
• 
INP – profiles: Only used in connection with the frame structures at the frame structures located on 
the 4th and 5th floor, respectively the West and East block. 
 
• 
Peikko Deltabeams: Used mostly at critical load conditions such as the deltabeam above the café in 
East block’s ground floor, where the deltabeam carries six floors of bearing walls with floor decks 
spanning on both sides. The entire length of a deltabeam must be cast simultaneously to obtain a 
composite beams effect. 
It is primarily concrete elements used as columns in this project, except at the frame structures, where INP 
– profiles have been implemented instead.  
The following types of concrete columns have been chosen: 
• 
Type 1 (square column: 400 x 400, T: 4Y16 + B: 4Y16):  
Used mostly for columns that pass through the entire building, requiring a larger bearing capacity. 
 
• 
Type 2 (square column: 400 x 400, T: 3Y16 + B: 3Y16):  
Only used in the basement except a single one on the ground floor. This type of column only carries 
a load from one storey. 
 
• 
Type 3 (column reinforced wall: 400 x 300, T: 4Y16 + B: 4Y16):  
Is a type of reinforced wall thickening used at the facade, where a large point load is located. 
 
                                                          
5 For instance Peikko’s element design program uses L/300 as the maximum allowable deformation

8.3.2 
Edge beams 
 
The main types of edge beams used in this project are HEB360 and HEM320. However, at two locations 
other profiles have been used. At one of the locations, a Peikko Deltabeam is implemented due to a large 
load and span, and at the other location, a HEM400 is implemented due to a large point load around the 
middle of the span and the deformation requirement. 
The HEB360 profiles are mainly used as edge beams, where the slabs do not span in their direction, while 
the HEM320 profiles are used as edge beams, where the floor decks span on them, resulting in a critical 
shear and deflection. 
In most cases, a HEB340 edge beam would have been sufficient, but instead the HEB360 profile was chosen 
to have the same height as the HEM320 profile. This result, in the areas where a HEB360 and a HEM320 
profile are connected, no height differences are present, resulting in a simple joint.  
In the following section, the three different edge beams will be presented. All the design loads can be 
found in Appendix D. 
8.3.2.1 
Edge beam location 
The location of the four presented profiles is shown in Figure 42. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:70==
Side 70 af 292 
 
 
 
Figure 42: Location of the presented edge beam profiles 
==PAGE:71==
Side 71 af 292 
 
 
8.3.2.2 
Edge beam verification 
8.3.2.2.1 E_0_N_EB1 + E_0_N_EB2 verification (HEM320) 
The design uniform load from the maximum load combination acting on E_0_N_EB1 is found to be 61 
kN/m, and the uniform characteristic load is found to 52 kN/m. These can be found in Appendix D on page 
173. 
The load acting on E_0_N_EB2, is divided into two parts: 
1. Part 1 is the facade load, which affects the entire beam length with a uniform load in the maximum 
load combination of 29 kN/m and a uniform characteristic load of 22 kN/m. These can be found in 
Appendix D on page 174. 
 
2. Part 2 is the load from the decks, which affects only the out hanging part (between the charnier and 
the third support, see Figure 43). The uniform load in the maximum load combination is found to be 
24 kN/m and the uniform characteristic load is found to be 19 kN/m. These can be found in 
Appendix D on page 175. 
The total span of E_0_N_EB1 is 10.8 m, and the total span of E_0_N_EB2 is 11.6 m. 
Edge beam E_0_N_EB1 and E_0_N_EB2 are designed to be HEM 320. These two beams have been verified 
in the same model. The reason why these two edge beams are modeled together in WinBeam (Figure 43) is 
because the connection between the beams is to be considered a charnier, which only transfers the shear 
force from one beam to the other and has the beams move together in that point instead of moving 
separately.  
 
Figure 43: Deflection curve of edge beams E_0_N_EB1 + E_0_N_EB2 caused by characteristic load combination 
The beams do not move separately in their connection, because, as seen in Figure 44, point C, the 
deformation at the out hanging part of each edge beam counters each other if the connection is made in 
such a way that it can transfer the shear force. 
 
Figure 44: Showing the deformation of edge beams E_0_N_EB1 (LEFT) + E_0_N_EB2 (RIGHT) 
When comparing the actual deflection and the allowable deflection of the beams, the allowable is larger 
than the actual. But this is without taking into consideration that the elements above these edge beams 
have holes in them to mount windows. This means that a verification of the actual deflection needs to be 
done after the window is mounted.  
==PAGE:72==
Side 72 af 292 
 
 
An assumption of the thickness of the joint between the windowsill and the window hole is made to be 10 
mm, which means that the allowable relative deformation after mounting the window cannot exceed this 
value, since it is assumed that the joint cannot deform more than these 10 mm. 
The reason why the relative deflection is controlled instead of the total deflection is that the walls, beams, 
and floor decks are set up before the window itself is mounted, allowing for their deflections to occur 
before the window is mounted. These existing deflections caused by the load from the walls, beams and 
floor decks are therefore ignored when checking the relative deflection. Instead, only the deflections 
occurring after the window has been mounted, such as the floor structure, moveable walls, installations 
and the live load, are taken into consideration. 
 
Figure 45: Relative deformation curve caused by loads after the window is mounted 
Seen in Figure 45, the relative deformation caused by the loads after the window is mounted is 7.1 mm, 
which is less than the 10 mm joint.  
In Figure 46, the moment curve of the maximum load combination is presented. The maximum moment is 
found to be 427 kNm, where the moment bearing capacity of a HEM 320 profile is 1742 kNm. This means 
that there is a utilization of approximately 25%. The moment capacity is therefore sufficient. 
The shear stress is found to be 72 MPa, and the shear capacity is 265 MPa. The shear capacity is therefore 
sufficient. 
 
Figure 46: Moment curve of E_0_N_EB1 + E_0_N_EB2 caused by the maximum load combination 
 
8.3.2.2.2 E_0_E_EB2 verification (HEB 360) 
A verification of the longest span of a HEB 360 is presented here with a total span of 9.2 m. 
The design uniform load from the maximum load combination acting on E_0_E_EB2 is found to be 24 kN/m, 
and the uniform characteristic load is found to be 18 kN/m. These can be found in Appendix D on page 177. 
In Figure 47, the moment curve of the maximum load combination is presented. The maximum moment is 
found to be 268 kNm, where the moment bearing capacity of a HEB 360 profile is 1100 kNm. This means 
that there is a utilization of approximately 25%. The moment capacity is therefore sufficient. 
==PAGE:73==
Side 73 af 292 
 
 
The shear stress is found to be 20 MPa, and the shear stress capacity is 265 MPa. The shear capacity is 
therefore sufficient. 
 
Figure 47: Moment curve of E_0_E_EB2 caused by the maximum load combination 
The total deflection is found to be 20 mm, and the allowable deflection is 23 mm. The total deflection is 
therefore sufficient. Since there is no deck span on the beam, it is not necessary to consider the relative 
deformation.  
 
Figure 48: Total deflection of E_0_E_EB2 caused by characteristic load combination 
The reason why such a relatively large profile was chosen for such a small load is the large deflection. 
8.3.2.2.3 W_0_N_EB2 verification (HEM400) 
A verification of a HEM400 is presented here with a total span of 6.5 m. 
This beam only carries the weight of the facade at the 1st floor and its self-weight. Furthermore, a column is 
placed on the beam approximately 40% of the span from the left. 
The design uniform load from the maximum load combination acting on W_0_N_EB2 is found to be 24 
kN/m, and the uniform characteristic load is found to be 18 kN/m. These can be found in Appendix D on 
page 180.  
Furthermore, the point load acting on the beam from the reaction of the column is calculated to 487 kN in 
the maximum load combination and 381 kN in the characteristic load combination. 
In Figure 49, the moment curve of the maximum load combination is presented. The maximum moment is 
found to be 908 kNm where the moment bearing capacity of a HEM 400 profile is 2210 kNm. This means 
that there is a utilization of approximately 41%. The moment capacity is therefore sufficient. 
 
Figure 49: Moment curve of W_0_N_EB2 caused by the maximum load combination 
==PAGE:74==
Side 74 af 292 
 
 
The reaction of the columns above the beam gives in a shear load of 667 kN in the maximum load 
combination (see Figure 50), which results in a shear stress of 97.4 MPa. The shear stress capacity of a HEM 
400 profile beam is 265 MPa. The shear capacity is therefore sufficient. 
 
Figure 50: Shear curve of W_0_N_EB2 caused by the maximum load combination 
The total deflection is found to be 12 mm, and the allowable deflection is 16,35 mm. The total deflection is 
therefore sufficient. Since there is no deck span on the beam, it is not necessary to consider the relative 
deformation.  
 
Figure 51: Total deflection of W_0_N_EB2 caused by characteristic load combination 
The reason why this edge beam is chosen specially apart from the other beams is due to the large point 
load from the columns, where there is a glass facade located on the ground floor below the beam. Due to 
aesthetical reasons, this prevents the placement of a column inside the glass facade for support.

8.3.4.2.1 E_0_B3 verification 
E_0_B3 is an out-hanging beam, which is an extension of the wall. This only occurs at the ground floor due 
to out-hanging facades. The beam has a total span of 3.5 m and an out-hanging part is 1.3 m. 
To achieve a realistic analysis, this wall beam has been chosen to be modeled in a FEM-program StruSoft, 
where it is possible to model a line support. See Figure 67 for applied design load and support conditions. 
 
  
Figure 67: Design load and support condition for wall beam E_0_B3 
 
In Figure 68, a negative moment of 408 kNm has been found. 
 
Figure 68: Moment curve of E_0_B3 
 
==PAGE:85==
Side 85 af 292

Figure 69: Shear curve of E_0_B3  
By using the beam calculation worksheet created by “Betonforeningen15”, the wall beam E_0_B3 has been 
given a geometry of 200x700 mm with two layers of 3Y20 at the top to resist the moment, and additional 
stirrups of Y8/100 along the span to resist the shear force.  
The reason for a larger amount of reinforcement at the top of the wall beam is the negative moment 
causing tension at the top of the wall beam. See Figure 70 for the design. 
 
Figure 70: E_0_B3 design

8.3.4.2.2 W_0_B8 verification 
A D40-400 deltabeam has been chosen as a wall beam (see Figure 76) instead of the previous wall beam 
solution. The previous wall beam would require a thickening of the wall due to the large shear force, which 
has been decided to be inconsistent with the overall aesthetics of the building. The beam spans 5.5 m with 
an out-hanging part of 1.5 m. 
By using a D40-400 deltabeam, no thickening is needed, but instead a special joint between the deltabeam 
and the wall is required. 
The design uniform load from the maximum load combination acting on W_0_B8 is found to be 90 kN/m, 
and the uniform characteristic load is found to be 76 kN/m. These can be found in Appendix D on page 200. 
In Figure 72, the moment curve of the maximum load combination is presented. The maximum moment is 
found to be 391.2 kNm, where the moment bearing capacity of a D40-400 profile is 1467 kNm16. This 
means that there is a utilization of approximately 27%. The moment capacity is therefore sufficient. 
 
 
Figure 72: Moment curve of W_0_B8 caused by the maximum load combination 
                                                          
16 Obtained from a Peikko supply data table, see Appendix L. 
==PAGE:87==
Side 87 af 292 
 
 
The shear load is found to be 645 kN in the maximum load combination (see Figure 73), while the D40-400 
profile has a shear load capacity of 973 kN17. The shear capacity is therefore sufficient. 
 
 
Figure 73: Shear curve of W_0_B8 caused by the maximum load combination 
The total deflection is found to be 2.2 mm (see Figure 65), and the allowable deflection is 3.9 mm. The total 
deflection is therefore sufficient. 
 
 
Figure 74 Total deflection of W_0_B8 caused by characteristic load combination 
The location of the supports has been chosen as shown in Figure 76, and the reactions at the supports can 
be seen in Figure 75. 
At the first and third support, a compression force is present. To avoid the crushing of the concrete wall 
due to the compression forces, a steel plate is placed between the deltabeam and the wall, and inside the 
wall below this plate a column reinforcement of 3Y16 + 3Y16 is used.  
At the second support, a tension force is present. To resist this tension force, two bonded anchors of bolt 
type Y20 are installed. The anchoring length of the bolts is calculated to be 720 mm.

8.4 SPECIAL STRUCTURES 
8.4.1 
Specially designed support for out-hanging floor deck  
Due to the retracted facade at the ground floor, it was a challenge to design an out-hanging floor deck. Two 
different design propositions were made to resist the moment and shear load caused by the bearing walls 
carried eccentrically above and through the building.  
First solution was a special insitu cast concrete floor deck (see Figure 84), which, in theory, was designed to 
have a sufficient moment and shear capacity to resist the eccentric load from the bearing walls. However, 
in reality an uncertainty concerning sufficient space for the stirrups inside the concrete floor deck was 
present. 
 
Figure 84: Location of the special deck (first solution) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:96==
Side 96 af 292 
 
 
This led to the second solution, which is also the chosen design used in this project. See illustration of 
structure in Figure 85. 
 
Figure 85: Illustration of the second solution 
The new structural design consists of a deck and facade carried by a PEIKKO Deltabeam DR50-350, which is 
simply supported by two edge beams HEM320 that are eccentrically supported by square concrete 
columns. This design proposition is present on each floor, allowing for the load to be distributed evenly one 
floor at a time to satisfy the deflection control. 
The design process of this structure first considers that the DR50-350 Deltabeam has a sufficient bearing 
capacity.  
A verification of the DR50-350 profile, which has a span of 11.5 m, is presented here. 
The design uniform load is found as a result of three different load intensities due to three different span 
lengths. The uniform load in the maximum load combination is found to be 93 kN/m (from module line ØK-
ØJ), 79 kN/m (from module line ØJ-ØH) and 64 kN/m (from module line ØH-ØG). 
The uniform load in the characteristic load combination is found to be 80 kN/m (from module line ØK-ØJ), 
58 kN/m (from module line ØJ-ØH) and 46 kN/m (from module line ØH-ØG). 
 
 
==PAGE:97==
Side 97 af 292 
 
 
In Figure 86, the moment curve of the maximum load combination is presented. The maximum moment is 
found to be 1290 kNm, where the moment bearing capacity of a DR50-350 profile is 3758 kNm. This means 
thay there is a utilization of approximately 34%. The moment capacity is therefore sufficient. 
 
Figure 86: Moment curve of E_0_E_EB5 caused by the maximum load combination 
The reaction on the edge beam results in a shear load of 483.7 kN (see Figure 87). The shear capacity of a 
DR50-350 profile is 1339 kN. The shear capacity is therefore sufficient. 
 
Figure 87: Shear curve of E_0_E_EB5 caused by the maximum load combination 
The total deflection is found to be 31.85 mm, and the allowable deflection is 38.4 mm. The total deflection 
is therefore sufficient. Since there is nothing below the beam, it is not necessary to consider the relative 
deformation.  
 
Figure 88: Total deflection of E_0_E_EB5 caused by characteristic load combination 
The reason why this DR50-350 profile was chosen specifically for this situation was due to the long span 
resulting in a large deflection. 
Secondly, the design process considers the HEM320 edge beams, which support the DR50-350 
eccentrically. 
A verification of the HEM320 profile with a span of 5.1 m is presented here. 
The design uniform load from the maximum load combination acting on this beam is found to be 32 kN/m, 
and the uniform characteristic load is found to be 25 kN/m. Furthermore, a point load, as a result of the 
reaction from the previously verified beam (E_0_E_EB5), is found to be 1780 kN in the maximum load 
combination. 
 
==PAGE:98==
Side 98 af 292 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 89: Moment curve of E_0_S_EB4 caused by the maximum load combination 
The reaction on the edge beam results in a shear load of 2028 kN (see Figure 87), resulting in a shear stress 
of 346 MPa. This means that the original HEM320 profile’s shear capacity is not sufficient enough to resist 
this shear stress, and instead a profile was tailored by using a HEM320 profile and adding two additional 
web plates at the edge of the flanges (see Figure 91). The additional plates are first mounted in-between 
the top and bottom flanges, and then a recess is made along the outer side of where the plates connect to 
the flanges, which is then welded. The shear stress is now reduced to 143 MPa, resulting in the shear 
capacity being sufficient. 
 
Figure 90: Shear curve of E_0_S_EB4 caused by the maximum load combination 
==PAGE:99==
Side 99 af 292 
 
 
 
Figure 91: Illustration of the edge beam with additional reinforced steel plates 
 
 
 
 
The total deflection is found to be 6.3 mm, and the allowable deflection is 13 mm. The total deflection is 
therefore sufficient.  
 
Figure 92: Total deflection of E_0_S_EB4 caused by characteristic load combination 
The verification of the edge beam supporting the DR50-350 profile on one side (E_0_N_EB5), is identical to  
edge beam on the other side (E_0_S_EB4).  
 
 
 
==PAGE:100==
Side 100 af 292 
 
 
8.4.2 
Stiffening system of the facade on roof terrace 
A stiffening system has been made for both the facade at West block’s roof terrace on the 4th floor, and the 
facade at East block’s roof terrace on the 5th floor.  
In the following section, a verification of the stiffening system for the facade on the roof terrace of the East 
Block will be done. See the location and a sketch of the stiffening system in Figure 9318.  
 
 
Figure 93: Location and sketch of the stiffening system at east block on 5th floor 
The stiffening system consists of a vertical steel profile and a horizontal steel profile. The vertical stiffener is 
welded to the horizontal stiffener, and the horizontal stiffener is simply supported on the floor deck. 
The static system of the stiffening system used for the roof terrace is considered a combination of a 
cantilevered column and a simply supported beam with a moment acting on support B (See Figure 94). 
 
Figure 94: Static system of stiffening system 
In Figure 95, an illustration of the division of the vertical stiffeners can be seen. The stiffeners are more 
densely placed at the area where the facade is highest and progressively becoming less dense the lower the 
facade height becomes. The idea behind this design is to maintain one profile along the facade, which is 
why instead of increasing the profile dimension, a reduction of the load is done by placing more stiffeners. 
This also coheres with the fact that a higher facade needs a longer stiffening profile, which leads to a larger 
moment arm. 
                                                          
18 The UNP-profile on top of the vertical stiffeners is not visible. 
==PAGE:101==
Side 101 af 292 
 
 
 
Figure 95: Illustration of division of the stiffeners 
On the top of these vertical stiffeners, a UNP profile is placed, which transports the facade load as a point 
load acting on the top of the vertical stiffeners. This results in a partial deflection of the vertical stiffener 
and a moment at the bottom of the vertical stiffeners. This moment forms a rotation in the horizontal 
stiffener causing an additional deflection. These two deflections together form the total deflection of the 
vertical stiffener. The total deflection of the stiffening system should be lower than the allowable deflection 
of the steel profile and the brick wall in the facade. 
The load from the reaction of the horizontal stiffener must not exceed the punching shear capacity of the 
floor deck, which is estimated to be 10 kN19. If the reaction (at support C) of the horizontal stiffener 
exceeded the punching shear capacity, the length of the horizontal stiffener should be increased to obtain a 
larger moment arm, which would result in a lower reaction. The reaction’s point of attack is always chosen 
to be in the middle of the floor deck, which means that the length of the horizontal stiffener is always 
extended by the width of a slab (1200 mm) from the original placement. 
Both the vertical stiffeners and the horizontal stiffeners are designed to be HEB180 profiles.  
See the verification in Appendix N. The corresponding length of the stiffeners can be seen in Table 32.  
The modeling of the UNP profile is done under an assumption of elastic behavior and a point load of 1 kN, 
which results in a deflection. The ratio between the point load and the deflection is considered a spring 
stiffness constant of the support. This spring stiffness is defined as input data in winbeam (see Table 32 and 
Figure 96) and used to verify the bearing capacity and deflection of the chosen UNP profile. The UNP profile 
chosen is UNP280.  
See Appendix N for the winbeam output for the modeling of the UNP profile.  
 
                                                          
19 Approximately 1/3 of the shear capacity of a hollow core slab 
==PAGE:102==
Side 102 af 292

8.5 ROBUSTNESS 
The robustness of the buildings will be verified in this section, designing the edge reinforcement and the 
joint reinforcement of the slabs. 
The floor decks above the basement are considered the most critical floor disc due to a combination of the 
horizontal mass load20 and the additional earth pressure. For all other floors, only the horizontal mass load 
acts on the floor disc, which is why the basement is the design case in this project. 
The earth pressure is calculated for both ultimate limit state and serviceability limit state, and the largest 
resulting reaction acting at the top of the wall is used to design the edge reinforcement of the floor disc 
above the basement. The largest load at the top of the wall is 36.96 kN/m and takes place in the 
serviceability limit state. The reason why the serviceability limit state is more critical than the ultimate limit 
state is that a compressed earth pressure is present.  
 
Figure 97: Load cases for the earth pressure in Ultimate limit state and Serviceability limit state 
In Figure 98, a division of the floor disc in the basement is presented, where the stabilizing walls (red lines) 
indicate the supports of the floor disc and the numbers 1-8 represent the different floor disc divisions. 
 
 
                                                          
20 The most critical horizontal load 
==PAGE:104==
Side 104 af 292 
 
 
 
Figure 98: Division of decks for the edge reinforcement in basement
==PAGE:105==
Side 105 af 292 
 
 
In the following, an edge reinforcement design of floor disc ① will be presented. 
A static system of the edge reinforcement design of floor disc ① is shown in Figure 99 and is considered a 
simply supported beam. 
 
Figure 99: Static system of the first division of the floor decks 
The load attacking along the disc is 38.4 kN/m, which is the sum of the largest acting load on the top of the 
wall from the earth pressure and the horizontal mass load redistributed along the disc.  
The acting load and the span along the disc result in a moment of 1229 kNm, which result in a tension force 
of 231 kN at the bottom; the force the edge reinforcement is designed to resist.  
By using 2Y20 as edge reinforcement, a capacity of 288 kN is achieved, which gives a utilization of 80%. The 
2Y20 edge reinforcement satisfies this situation.  
The most critical division is floor disc ⑦ due to its long span. 2Y20 is still satisfactory in this case (see 
calculation in Appendix O). However, an extra edge reinforcement should be placed at every 4th floor deck 
at the edge of the disc. The reason for this is that during the erection phase, a crane is placed in the middle 
of the disc, which results in a hole through the floor discs above, and if the extra edge reinforcement is not 
placed, the floor disc will not be able to achieve a disc effect, which is needed to transfer the load from one 
edge to the opposite edge.  
2Y20 will be used as edge reinforcement in the floor disc above the basement. 
For all other floors it has been assumed that the minimum edge reinforcement is applied due to the lack of 
earth pressure.  
The joint reinforcement between all decks has been chosen to be 2Y12.  
Considering the possibility of an explosion load, a tension connection should be established between all the 
vertical structural member connections to avoid lifting of the elements.

10 CONCLUSION 
The building is designed according to Eurocode and Danish national indexes with additional notes.  
A load specification is given according to the usage of the different rooms and consist of the elements’ self-
weight, diverse other self-weights, imposed loads from the floor decks and nature loads. A load diagram 
over the entire building is then made to get an overview of the surface loads and to be used when 
calculating the vertical load distribution. 
To calculate the vertical load distribution, the surface loads are set into a load matrix, while the bearing line 
is set into a load vector.  Based on this, a worksheet is constructed, where 16 load combinations can be 
obtained by inputting the surface load type, deck span length and bearing line type as data. This worksheet 
is used to find the design load on all the structural members throughout the entire project.  In most of the 
design cases, the dominating imposed load was the most critical load case.  
The floor decks in the entire building are designed to be hollow core slab EX220 except at the areas outside 
the building area above the parking facility, where TT60/600 slabs are used.   
The horizontal load is calculated in both the wind case and the horizontal mass load case. The most critical 
of these two cases was the horizontal mass load. This horizontal mass load is used to design the stabilizing 
walls and the edge reinforcement for the structures. The horizontal mass load is distributed through the 
floor decks and to the stabilizing walls by using the generalized method. The wall stability has been 
investigated with the Strusoft FEM-program. The building layout results in no stability issues. However, 
tension connections in the form of corrugated tubes are still applied due the robustness of the buildings. 
The load intensity from the compression zone obtained from the stability analysis is less critical than the 
vertical load distribution. The vertical load distribution is therefore used to design the wall reinforcement. 
This resulted in a net reinforcement of Y12/150 in both directions on both sides for both the walls with a 
thickness of 150 mm and a thickness of 200 mm. 
HEB and HEM profiles are primarily used as edge beams, which carry the load from the facade at each floor. 
The beams used inside the building area are a combination of HEB, HEM and welded steel profiles. 
Concrete columns are designed with the corresponding amount reinforcements.  
Due to the architectural design, a stiffening system is required to stabilize the facade at the roof terrace. 
The stiffening system consists of a vertical stiffener, which is welded to a horizontal stiffener, and a UNP 
profile placed at the top of the vertical stiffener to transfer the load from the facade as a point load. The 
horizontal stiffener is bolted to the floor deck at a specified distance to limit the reaction, so that it does 
not exceed the punching shear capacity of the floor deck.  
The edge reinforcement is designed based on the most critical horizontal load, which are the horizontal 
mass load and the earth pressure. The floor decks above the basement from where the earth pressure acts 
in combination with the horizontal mass load, the edge reinforcement has been designed to be 2Y20 for 
every fourth floor deck element. This allows the entire floor deck to work as a disc with the ability to 
transfer the load from one edge to another. For all other floors above, 2Y16 is applied instead at every 4th 
floor deck element to gain the same disc effect. 
The roof structure and foundation have not been designed in this project.

Side 117 af 292
